[2026-01-24T23:29:39Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: Q.	Václav Havel was a X dissident when this country was ruled by a puppet regime allied with the Soviet Union. In 1978, he wrote the essay “The Power of the Powerless.” In it, he talks about a greengrocer who puts a sign in his shop window that says “workers of the world, unite!”, which is a quote from the book Y. Havel wrote that we can safely assume that the overwhelming majority of shopkeepers never think about the slogans that they put in their windows, nor do they use them to express their real opinions. With this parable, Havel explained how authoritarian regimes endure not just through repression but also through routine acts of compliance by citizens. He called this kind of outward conformance with the system’s rituals, slogans, and behaviors “A within the B.” He then argued that the solution to this would be for ordinary people to start “A in C” to puncture the narratives of the system and make it clear to other people that they all don’t believe them. ID X, Y, A, B, and C. B and C are antonyms.

[2026-01-25T22:47:28Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: Q.	Central banks issuing banknotes must ensure that they keep meticulous track of how many notes are printed, so that they can control money supply in the economy, a key tool in monetary policy. Modern high-speed printing presses used to manufacture currency notes in the United States and India require serial numbers to be in a strict mathematical sequence. When Quality Control inspects each sheet for errors; if any errors are found, the entire sheet is discarded. Stopping the press to reprint notes with those same serial numbers as the discarded ones would be very inefficient. Instead, a pre-printed sheet with different serial numbers and a X next to the serial number is inserted in place of the discarded sheet. Because printing errors are rare, notes with a X sign on them are rare, which make them attractive to collectors, increasing their value. Collectors call them “X notes.” ID X.

[2026-01-25T23:53:38Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: Q.	A wind rose is a diagram used by meteorologists to give a succinct view of how wind speed and direction are typically distributed at a particular location. Wind roses were predecessors of the compass rose, found on nautical charts. Using a polar coordinate system of gridding, the frequency of winds over a time period is plotted by wind direction, with colour bands showing wind speed ranges. The direction of the longest spoke shows the wind direction with the greatest frequency, the prevailing wind. Wind roses are essential when making the most important decisions about X design.
